♠ Alternative names ♣ Wagadu Late Period; Ghana; Kingdom of Ghana; Ghana Empire; Awkar; Janawa; Aoukar ♥ "Ghana is a title given to their kings; the name of the region is Awkar."[2] Soninke. Wagadu.[3] "Ghana is a title given to their kings; the name of the region is Awkar."[4] Al-Zuhri (c1130-1155 CE) "considered Ghana the capital of the Janawa" [5]

"Wagadu, known to the Berber traders of the Saharan market centres as Aoukar. But the world came to know it by the title of its king, which was Ghana."[6]

♠ Peak Date ♣ ♥

"With the decline of the Almoravids in the twelfth century, Ghana became the richest kingdom in the Sudan, but in the thirteenth century its former tributaries freed themselves from central control, and the kingdom disintegrated. The decline of Ghana gave rise to a number of small states among Soninke-speaking peoples." [7]

Temporal bounds[edit]

♠ Duration ♣ 1078-1203 CE ♥

collapsed 1203 CE, after the Sosso took the capital, Kumbi Saleh. There seems to be a consensus within French-language scholarship that the Ghana empire didn't fall to the Soussou in 1203. Instead, they suggest it gradually collapsed at the end of the 11th century, and no longer played an important commercial role from the 13th century onwards. [8]

♠ Degree of centralization ♣ unitary state ♥

"The persistently arid conditions which for centuries had been a primary determinant of human population trends and movements in sub-Saharan Africa yielded to more amenable conditions around AD 300. Rainfall increased and became plentiful during the period up to about AD 1100, promoting the expansion of both local and long-distance trade networks. Population densities increased too - of both humans and livestock - and a conjunction of internal and external influences transformed the political structure of some ethnic groups from the age-set system which dispersed authority through the community to a system favouring centralized control and the formation of states."[9]

"the only area in which we can convincingly assert that a kingdom existed in the period under review was at the western edge of the Sudan, where the kingdom of Ghana was certainly in existence by +700 and could have been emerging for up to a thousand years."[10]

Here too there was a town where the king of Ghana lived, and another town nearby where the Muslim traders had their houses and stables."[15]

General Description[edit]

The Kingdom of Ghana was the first documented empire of West Africa. Its dominant people, a northern Mande group known as the Soninke, called it 'Wagadu',[18] and Berber traders from the Sahara referred to it as 'Awkar'.[19] The polity reached its peak in the mid-11th century:[20][21] at this stage, its influence extended east and north from the Senegal River into modern Mauritania and Mali[22] and it was encroaching on the Niger Inland Delta.[23][24] However, from the late 11th century CE the Ghana Empire began to decline due to a combination of environmental, social and political factors. As the desert expanded into previously productive agricultural land,[25] the Sosso people took control of a large region above the Upper Niger River[26] and the city of Walata grew in influence, taking over as the main southern terminus of the trans-Saharan trade.[27] The empire of Ghana officially converted to Islam in 1075‒77 CE to foster political and commercial ties with Almoravid newcomers,[28] while traditional religion retained its prominence in the hinterland and among the non-elite classes.[29] The consensus within modern scholarship is that the relationship between the Soninke and the Almoravids was generally cordial and fruitful, that the Wagadu capital had been host to an important Muslim community in the centuries before the Almoravids arrived, and that the Wagadu conversion to Islam was in fact a gradual affair.[30] Ghana recovered part of its power in the 12th century.[31] Ultimately, however, the shift in the regional balance of power led many of the Soninke to relocate to more prosperous areas,[32] contributing to the spread of Islam in other areas of the Sudan.[33]

Population and political organization

The Wagadu empire comprised four provinces administered by a central government.[34] The king exerted direct authority over his kingdom; he was also head of the traditional religion and was revered as a god.[35] Wagadu society was highly hierarchical, distinguishing between the elite warrior class and the rest of the population: professional artisans including smiths, weavers, dyers and shoemakers; farmers and herders; and slaves.[36] These groups were further subdivided along clan lines.[37]
This period was a prosperous one for the Sudanese region, which produced millet, maize, yam, groundnuts, cotton, indigo and other crops.[38] Linked into a thriving exchange sphere that stretched north to North Africa and the Mediterranean,[39] the Sudanese population exported gold, slaves, hides, and ivory and imported copper, silver beads, dried fruit and cloth.[40] All exports and imports were taxed by the centralized state.[41] Trading outposts in Awdhagust and other Saharan towns facilitated fruitful exchange with Berbers and other groups from further afield.[42]
Population estimates are difficult to obtain for ancient Ghana. However, it is worth noting that its capital, the thriving trading city of Kumbi Saleh, covered 250 hectares and had a population of 15,000-20,000 people at its peak.[43] Archaeological investigations at the site have revealed two-storey stone buildings which may have contained stores on the ground floor, narrow streets with densely packed houses, a mosque, and extensive cemeteries.[44]

Social Scale[edit]

♠ Polity territory ♣ [350,000-400,000] ♥ in squared kilometers

Estimated from a map showing the Soninke homeland [47] and another map which shows Ghana also extending further east and south into the Niger Inland Delta region.[48]

"Among the provinces of Ghana is a region called Sama, the inhabitants of which are known as the Bukum. From that region to Ghana is four day's travel."[49]

Greatest territorial extent: Djaka on the west of the Niger River to the Atlantic Ocean, and north to south, from the Sahara to the edge of Mali. The gold-rich region of Upper Senegal, centered around Gadiaru, Garentel, and Iresni, belonged to the Empire."[50]

♠ Polity Population ♣ [1,000,000-1,500,000] ♥ People.

Territory of "Gana" in 1000 CE included the Inland Delta region of Mali from Timbuktu to the tributaries/uplands, the eastern half of Mauritania and part of eastern Senegal.[51] We need an estimate of the population within this region. Using the McEvedy and Jones figure of 2 million by 1000 CE for the "Sahel States" (Mauritania, Mali, Niger, Chad) I will estimate about 1-1.5 million.

"Before the introduction of agriculture and animal husbandry the population of the area of the present-day Sahel states is unlikely to have exceeded 50,000: once pastoralism and agriculture had become well-established the population can hardly have been less than half a million. The chronology of the transition is as yet totally obscure, but there is no reason to postulate anything above the 50,000 line before 3000 BC or place the achievement of the half million later than 1000 BC. From this latter point a low rate of increase is all that is needed to bring the total to 1m by AD 1 and 2m by AD 1000." Sahal states 2.2m 1100 CE, 2.4m 1200 CE, 2.6m 1300 CE, 2.8m 1400 CE, 3m 1500 CE 3.5m 1600 CE. [52]


♠ Population of the largest settlement ♣ [15,000-20,000] ♥ Inhabitants.

Will assume estimate for Koumbi Saleh refers to this period since the same author earlier said that "Timbuktu was already a trading centre of notable size in the eighth century AD."[53] If we used the hectare coverage to provide an estimate 200 per ha for 250 hectares would give us 50,000 people. However, we do not know if all the hectares were occupied at the same time so will go with the previous numerical estimate for the same place made by the same author.

15,000-20,000

"occupied from the sixth to the eighteenth century AD and home to between 15,000 and 20,000 people when it was most densely inhabited."[54] -- when was Koumbi Saleh most densely inhabited?

250 hectares

Koumbi Saleh was a city in Ancient Ghana. "excavations and aerial surveys have revealed the remains of a large town covering an area of about 250 hectares with stone buildings, some of them two storeys high, the ground floors of which appear to have been used as stores for merchandise. The houses were close together, the streets narrow; there was a mosque, and extensive cemeteries." [55]

"The city of Ghana consists of two towns situated on a plain."[56]

"Le royaume couvrait les villes de Bokounou, Ouagadou et de Kaarta." The kingdom covered the cities of Bokounou , Ouagadou and Kaarta[57]

"Timbuktu was already a trading centre of notable size in the eighth century AD." "...its subsequent growth and status is almost entirely attributable to the salt that the Tuareg camel caravans brought to its markets. From the backs of camels the salt was transhipped to canoes for distribution through the hundreds of kilometres of navigable waters on the Niger River system."[58]

Kumbi Saleh in 1240 CE had "as many as 15,000 inhabitants or even more."[59]

♠ Largest communication distance ♣ [300-350] ♥ in kilometers.

Al-Bakri shows that the authority of Ghana, at its peak in the 11th century, didn't extend further than the distance that could be covered by a six day walk to the West and three day walk to the East. [60]

If we take this literally: 5 kph (walking speed) * (24 hrs *6) = 720 km. It is probably unreasonable to suggest that Al-Bakri meant six 24 hour days. If we assume 12 hr days then we get a figure of 360 km.

On the coding sheet of the previous Wagadu polity I estimated Koumbi Saleh to Walatta? 311 km. So 300-350km could be roughly correct.


Hierarchical Complexity[edit]

♠ Settlement hierarchy ♣ 4 ♥ levels.

1. Capital town

2. Provincial town
"L’empire était subdivisé en royaumes et en provinces eux-mêmes morcelés en villages et cantons." (The empire was divided into kingdoms and provinces themselves broken up into villages and townships).[61]
3. Small agricultural villages
"Sudanic societies were built on small agricultural villages or herding communities, sometimes but not always integrated into larger tribal and linguistic groups." [62]
"By about 100 B.C.E., the Soninke's ancestors began establishing small settled communities, and around 600 B.C.E. these grew into large villages administered by chieftains."[63]


♠ Administrative levels ♣ 4 ♥ levels.

1. King

According to oral tradition, the Cisse was the ruling clan of Wagadu. Ruler had the title "maghan." [64]
In earlier times there may have been "matrilineal descent (power passed to the son of the king's sister)" and "There might even have been instances of female chieftains." [65]
"Ghana is a title given to their kings"[66]


_Central court_

2. Head official of the General Council
"L’autorité du roi et de son gouvernement central s’exerçait de façon directe sur le berceau originel du royaume soninké." (The authority of the king and his central government was exercised directly from the original birthplace of the Soninke kingdom).[67]
The most powerful aristocratic clans were collectively known as wago. "That term, and the name of the kingdom, Wagadu, are probably related. "Wagadu" is a contraction of wagadugu, which can be translated as "land of the wago"."[68]
"La société était organisée en clans. Le clan royal était celui des Tounkara qui formaient avec trois autres clans l’aristocratie:( les Souba ou Magasouba étaient les guerriers du roi, les Kagoro qui formaient une élite militaire, les Magassi étaient les cavaliers du roi qui composaient la garde royale.). Ces clans qui constituent la noblesse fournissaient au roi, les grands dignitaires et hauts fonctionnaires de sa cour. On trouvait à la cour du roi, le gouvernement et le grand conseil dont les membres se recrutaient aussi bien dans l’aristocratie locale que chez les arabes et les lettrés musulmans. On trouvait au sein de son gouvernement, les fils des rois vassaux, otages à la cour. La succession sur le trône se faisait d’oncle à neveu." (The society was organized in clans. The royal clan was that of Tounkara who formed with three other aristocratic clans: (the Souba or Magasouba were the warriors of the king, the Kagoro who formed a military elite, the riders were Magassi king composing the royal guard). These clans that make up the nobility provided the king, the great dignitaries and senior officials of his court. It was at the king's court, the government and the general council whose members were recruited in both the local aristocracy among Arab and Muslim scholars. It was within his government, son of the vassal kings, hostages to the court. The succession to the throne was uncle to nephew.[69]
3. Treasury official
"The king has a palace and a number of domed dwellings all surrounded with an enclosure like a city wall. ... The king's interpreters, the official in charge of his treasury and the majority of his ministers are Muslims."[70]
4. Scribes
"For the sake of administrative support, legitimization, and commercial contacts, the rulers of Kawkaw, Takrur, Ghana, and Bornu adopted Islam in the late tenth and eleventh centuries. Islam became an imperial cult and the religion of state and trading elites, while the agricultural populations maintained their traditional beliefs." [71]


"A Sudanic empire commonly had a core territory integrated by ethnic, linguistic, or similar ties and a larger sphere of power defined by the rule of a particular person or lineage over numerous subordinate families, castes, lineages, and village communities. The key political factor was not the control of territory but the relations that enabled the ruler to garner religious prestige, draw military support, and extract taxes or tributes. The kings were considered sacred persons and were believed to have divine powers. They did not appear in public and were not to be seen carrying out ordinary bodily functions such as eating. Around the kings were numerous officeholders who helped govern the realm and provincial and district chiefs often recruited from junior members of the noble families."[72]


_Regional government_


2. Princes (governors called fado) of a province
"L’empire était subdivisé en royaumes et en provinces eux-mêmes morcelés en villages et cantons." (The empire was divided into kingdoms and provinces themselves broken up into villages and townships).[73]
"Les princes avaient en charge la gestion des provinces tandis que les royaumes vassaux tels que Sosso, Diara et le Tékrour conservaient leur organisation initiale et se contentaient de verser un tribut annuel et d’apporter leur contribution sur le plan militaire en fournissant à l’empereur un contingent." (The princes had control over the management of the provinces while the vassal kingdoms such as Sosso, Diara and Tekrour retained their initial organization and were happy to pay an annual tribute and to contribute militarily by providing the Emperor a quota).[74]
According to oral tradition there were four provinces, whose governors/commanders (dual military and administrative powers implied) were known as fado. Ruler had the title "maghan." [75]
Al-Bakri 1068 CE: king's city had a governor [76]
"Among the provinces of Ghana is a region called Sama, the inhabitants of which are known as the Bukum. From that region to Ghana is four day's travel."[77]
3. Village chief
"L’empire était subdivisé en royaumes et en provinces eux-mêmes morcelés en villages et cantons." (The empire was divided into kingdoms and provinces themselves broken up into villages and townships).[78]
4. Townships
"L’empire était subdivisé en royaumes et en provinces eux-mêmes morcelés en villages et cantons." (The empire was divided into kingdoms and provinces themselves broken up into villages and townships).[79]


_Vassal Kingdoms_

2. King
Ahmad al-Yaqubi (d. 897) said Ghana's king had "lesser kings under his authority."[80]
Al-Bakri 1068 CE: king of Ghana had vassal kings [81]
"Les princes avaient en charge la gestion des provinces tandis que les royaumes vassaux tels que Sosso, Diara et le Tékrour conservaient leur organisation initiale et se contentaient de verser un tribut annuel et d’apporter leur contribution sur le plan militaire en fournissant à l’empereur un contingent." (The princes had control over the management of the provinces while the vassal kingdoms such as Sosso, Diara and Tekrour retained their initial organization and were happy to pay an annual tribute and to contribute militarily by providing the Emperor a quota).[82]
"On peut distinguer deux groupes composant le peuplement de l’empire: un au Nord et l’autre au Sud. Les gens du Nord se composent des tribus nomades berbères ou Touaregs (Les berbères Macmouda au sud du Maroc, les Zenâta), les Sanhadja ( les Goddala, les Messoufa, les Lemtouma spécialistes du désert.). Le groupe Sud comporte deux fractions: les Mazzara composés de Lebou, Wolof, Toucouleur, Sérères) et les Bafours (Soninké ou Ouakaré, les Marka, les Bambaras, les Malinké, les Songhaï.)." There can be distinguished two peoples within the empire ... Northerners consist of Berber Tuareg nomads (Berber Macmouda in southern Morocco, the Zenâta) and Sanhadja (the Goddala the Messoufa the Lemtouma specialists of the desert). The southern group included two fractions: the Mazzara composed of Lebu, Wolof, Toucouleur, Serere) and Bafour (Soninké or Ouakaré, Marka, Bambara, Malinke, Songhai ).[83]


♠ Religious levels ♣ 3 ♥ levels. Islam. "In former times the people of this country professed paganism until the year 469/1076-1077 when Yahya b. Abu Bakr the amir of Masufa made his appearance."[84]

_until 1077 CE_

1. King

"Their religion is paganism and the worship of idols. When their king dies they construct over the place where his tomb will be an enormous dome of acacia wood."[87]
2. Sorcerers
"In the king's town and not far from his court of justice, is a mosque where the Muslims who arrive at his court pray. Around the king's town are domed buildings and groves and thickets where the sorcerers of these people, men in charge of the religious cult, live."[88]
 ?. Heads of clans
"the basic social and political unit appears in the past to have been the small local group, bound together by ties of kinship. When a number of groups came together they formed a clan. The heads of local clans were usually responsible for certain religious rites connected with the land." [89]


King was supreme judge

"Au sommet de l’État, on a le roi; on le désigne sous plusieurs appellations « Kaya Maghan » qui signifie roi de l’or en langue Ouakaré, « Tounka » qui veut dire Seigneur ou Dieu. Ses pouvoirs étaient très étendus: il était le juge suprême. Il rendait la justice en tenant compte de l’appartenance religieuse. Ses sujets qui dans l’ensemble appartenaient à la religion traditionnelle étaient jugés selon la coutume,les musulmans, eux, l’étaient sur la base du Coran." At the top of the state, was the King; means the under several names "Kaya Maghan" meaning gold king in language Ouakaré "Tounka" meaning Lord or God. His powers were very extensive: he was the supreme judge. He dispensed justice in the light of religious affiliation. His subjects in all belonged to the traditional religion were judged according to custom, Muslims, themselves, were based on the Koran.[90]


♠ Military levels ♣ [3-4] ♥ levels.

Only reference to professional and standing army in early West Africa is Askia Muhammed Toure (r.1493-1529 CE) of the Songhai Empire who "created a professional full-time army" [91] and "standing army"[92]

1. King

2. Loyal clan leaders inferred
3. intermediate level? inferred
4. Individual soldier

"The kingdoms which began to emerge in the Sudan about the end of the first millennium and the great 'empires' - Ghana, Mali, Songhai - which played so large a part in the medieval history of West Africa differed in many ways from the modern nation-state. One must not think of them as compact and homogenous units. 'The Sudanese Empire', Trimingham has pointed out, 'was an amorphous agglomeration of kin-groups having little in common except mythical recognition of a far-off suzerain.' Such empires had no precise boundaries, for 'the ruler was not interested in dominating territory as such, but in relationship with social groups upon whom he could draw to provide levies in time of war, servants for his courts and cultivators to keep his granaries full.'" [93]


Professions[edit]

♠ Occupational complexity ♣ ♥ Iron-working technology developed in West Africa 500-400 BCE. [94] Iron Age from 600 BCE in West Africa (e.g. Benue valley in Nigeria and upper Niger River) "the development and spread of the basic technologies of metal production and the forging and smithing of metal tools, notably in iron."[95]

♠ Professional military officers ♣ inferred absent ♥ Only reference to professional and standing army in early West Africa is Askia Muhammed Toure (r.1493-1529 CE) of the Songhai Empire who "created a professional full-time army" [96] and "standing army"[97] before Askia Muhammad of Songhay Empire "Chiefs, kings and emperors of earlier times had relied on simply 'calling up' their subjects, their vassals, or their allies. ... But these were temporary armies. They were amateur armies. They served for a campaign or a war, and then everyone went home again until the next one."[98]

♠ Professional soldiers ♣ absent ♥ Only reference to professional and standing army in early West Africa is Askia Muhammed Toure (r.1493-1529 CE) of the Songhai Empire who "created a professional full-time army" [99] and "standing army"[100] before Askia Muhammad of Songhay Empire "Chiefs, kings and emperors of earlier times had relied on simply 'calling up' their subjects, their vassals, or their allies. ... But these were temporary armies. They were amateur armies. They served for a campaign or a war, and then everyone went home again until the next one."[101]

Distinction between people and army during Songhai period: "beginning with the reign of Askia Mohammad ... Instead of mass conscription, a permanent army was created; civilians who were not part of it could go about their business."[102]

♠ Professional priesthood ♣ present ♥ Salaried imams.

"The city of Ghana consists of two towns situated on a plain. One of those towns, which is inhabited by Muslims is large and possesses twelve mosques... There are salaried imams and muezzin, as well as jurists and scholars."[103]

"In the king's town and not far from his court of justice, is a mosque where the Muslims who arrive at his court pray. Around the king's town are domed buildings and groves and thickets where the sorcerers of these people, men in charge of the religious cult, live."[104]

Bureaucracy characteristics[edit]

♠ Full-time bureaucrats ♣ [absent; present] ♥

In West Africa no centralised professional bureaucracy likely existed before the reign of Askia Mohammed of the Songhai Empire.[105] Government officials existed before this time but they may not have been professional.

In Kumbi-Saleh there were "mosques and religious functionaries including imams, muezzins, Quran reciters, and scholars. The Muslims provided the ruler with interpreters and officials." [106] Imams could not be full-time bureaucrats.

Al-Bakri described what might be an incipient bureaucratic center: "The king has a palace and a number of domed dwellings all surrounded with an enclosure like a city wall. ... The king's interpreters, the official in charge of his treasury and the majority of his ministers are Muslims."[107]

First explicit mention of "central bureaucracy" Askia Muhammed Toure (r.1493-1529 CE) who "supported by Mande clans ... created a ... central bureaucracy."[108]

♠ Source of support ♣ ♥

♠ Examination system ♣ inferred absent ♥

♠ Merit promotion ♣ suspected unknown ♥ The king employed Muslims who were not of the official faith or, one must imagine, related to him. This implies they were hired for their ability to organize not because of nepotism or bribery.

♠ Specialized government buildings ♣ inferred present ♥ Al-Bakri described what might be an incipient bureaucratic center: "The king has a palace and a number of domed dwellings all surrounded with an enclosure like a city wall."[109]

Law[edit]

♠ Formal legal code ♣ present ♥ Islamic law. "In former times the people of this country professed paganism until the year 469/1076-1077 when Yahya b. Abu Bakr the amir of Masufa made his appearance."[110]

"Au sommet de l’État, on a le roi; on le désigne sous plusieurs appellations « Kaya Maghan » qui signifie roi de l’or en langue Ouakaré, « Tounka » qui veut dire Seigneur ou Dieu. Ses pouvoirs étaient très étendus: il était le juge suprême. Il rendait la justice en tenant compte de l’appartenance religieuse. Ses sujets qui dans l’ensemble appartenaient à la religion traditionnelle étaient jugés selon la coutume,les musulmans, eux, l’étaient sur la base du Coran." At the top of the state, was the King; means the under several names "Kaya Maghan" meaning gold king in language Ouakaré "Tounka" meaning Lord or God. His powers were very extensive: he was the supreme judge. He dispensed justice in the light of religious affiliation. His subjects in all belonged to the traditional religion were judged according to custom, Muslims, themselves, were based on the Koran. [111]

♠ Judges ♣ present ♥ "In former times the people of this country professed paganism until the year 469/1076-1077 when Yahya b. Abu Bakr the amir of Masufa made his appearance."[112] "The city of Ghana consists of two towns situated on a plain. One of those towns, which is inhabited by Muslims is large and possesses twelve mosques... There are salaried imams and muezzin, as well as jurists and scholars."[113]

The cadi was a Muslim judge appointed by the king who "handled mainly common-law misdemeanors, disputes between citizens, or between citizens and foreigners."[114]

♠ Courts ♣ present ♥ "In former times the people of this country professed paganism until the year 469/1076-1077 when Yahya b. Abu Bakr the amir of Masufa made his appearance."[115] "In the king's town and not far from his court of justice..."[116]

♠ Professional Lawyers ♣ present ♥ "In former times the people of this country professed paganism until the year 469/1076-1077 when Yahya b. Abu Bakr the amir of Masufa made his appearance."[117] "Today they are Muslims and have scholars, lawyers, and Koran readers and have become pre-eminent in these fields. Some of their chief leaders have come to al-Andalus... They have traveled to Makka ... and returned to their land to spend large sums on the Holy War."[118]

Al-Bakri described what might be an incipient bureaucratic center which may have contained an office of records: "The king has a palace and a number of domed dwellings all surrounded with an enclosure like a city wall. ... The king's interpreters, the official in charge of his treasury and the majority of his ministers are Muslims."[124]


Transport infrastructure[edit]

♠ Roads ♣ present ♥ Ibn Battuta (14th century) on the African interior said: "there is no need to travel by caravan, for the roads are that secure."[125] road from Ghana (city) to Ghiyaru [126]
♠ Bridges ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Canals ♣ inferred absent ♥ Only mention of a canal was a project started by abandoned by a Songhai king.
♠ Ports ♣ present ♥ "The middle section of the Niger, linking Timbuktu to Djenne (about 400 km upstream), and to Gao (about the same distance downstream), was the busiest inland waterway in West Africa... With its development, water transport transformed the middle Niger into one of the great centres of indigenous trade in Africa. It encouraged the growth of specialized occupations, such as the building and operation of canoes; it lead to the development of specialized ports on the water-ways; and it contributed to the political and economic homogeneity of the region." [127] "Kabara was the true military and commercial port through which all goods were exported from Timbuktu, to Djenne, Mali, and the Upper Niger in general, or Tirekka, Gao, and Tademekka, Kukia and the Dendi country, that is, present-day Upper Dahomey (Benin)."[128]

Measurement System[edit]

♠ Length ♣ inferred present ♥ The literate Muslims would have had a measurement system.
♠ Area ♣ inferred present ♥ The literate Muslims would have had a measurement system.
♠ Volume ♣ inferred present ♥ Cowrie shells "can be accurately traded by weight, by volume, and by counting; their colour and lustre do not fade as their durability compares favourably with that of metal coins."[133] does this apply to the Niger Inland Delta polities? "After about AD 900 the standard measure of value in North Africa became the Almoravid dinar, sometimes in West Africa called the mitcal. Although West Africans continued to use their own standards for measuring value - the cowrie shell became the most important of these - they also began measuring in dinars or mitcals when they were trading with North Africans."[134]
♠ Weight ♣ present ♥ Artefacts from Koumbi Saleh include "glass weights so small in size they must have been used for weighing gold."[135] Cowrie shells "can be accurately traded by weight, by volume, and by counting; their colour and lustre do not fade as their durability compares favourably with that of metal coins."[136] does this apply to the Niger Inland Delta polities? "After about AD 900 the standard measure of value in North Africa became the Almoravid dinar, sometimes in West Africa called the mitcal. Although West Africans continued to use their own standards for measuring value - the cowrie shell became the most important of these - they also began measuring in dinars or mitcals when they were trading with North Africans."[137]

Writing System[edit]

♠ Mnemonic devices ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Nonwritten records ♣ present ♥ The Soninke people have oral traditions, such as the "Legend of Wagadu" which is told by gesere who are "professional storytellers and musicians." [138]
♠ Written records ♣ present ♥ "There are no written records of any description to throw light on the history of West Africa before 900 A.D." [139] "The West Africans who laid the foundations of their medieval empires during the centuries before 900 C.E. did not develop a written language they could use to record historical events."[140] Oldest example of writing in West Africa c1100 CE tomb inscription at Gao.[141]
♠ Script ♣ present ♥ Artefacts from Koumbi Saleh include "stones inscribed with Koranic verses".[142]
♠ Non-phonetic writing ♣ inferred absent ♥ "There are no written records of any description to throw light on the history of West Africa before 900 A.D." [143] "The West Africans who laid the foundations of their medieval empires during the centuries before 900 C.E. did not develop a written language they could use to record historical events."[144] Oldest example of writing in West Africa c1100 CE tomb inscription at Gao.[145]
♠ Phonetic alphabetic writing ♣ inferred present ♥ Classic Arabic of Koran. "There are no written records of any description to throw light on the history of West Africa before 900 A.D." [146] "The West Africans who laid the foundations of their medieval empires during the centuries before 900 C.E. did not develop a written language they could use to record historical events."[147] Oldest example of writing in West Africa c1100 CE tomb inscription at Gao.[148]

Money[edit]

♠ Articles ♣ present ♥ Barter "at the periphery of the African kingdoms, some backwards tribes, such as the Lem-Lem in Southwest Ghana, perhaps on the banks of the present-day Faleme River, had been carrying on barter trade since the Carthaginian period."[152] This was where, without any direct contact, Carthaginian and Arab traders exchanged their goods for gold dust. However, this simple form of economy was not characteristic of the economies of the polities of these times.[153] barter economy and no professional merchants. "The non-essential items and foreign durables found at sites remote from their point of origin were traded from village to village, in relays, as part of what was certainly a vigorous trade in essential goods between local centres." [154]
♠ Tokens ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "Contemporary shipping contracts indicate that the Portuguese introduced the cowrie shell to West African commerce just after 1515 at the latest" . Cowrie used as medium-of-exchange. [155] Cowrie shells functioned as money. "The shells can be accurately traded by weight, by volume, and by counting; their colour and lustre do not fade as their durability compares favourably with that of metal coins."[156] Cowries at Awdaghurst "in the ninth to tenth centuries.... trading in them in the north in the eleventh century." [157] "D. Robert thinks that Awdaghurst may have been the source of the copper wire used as 'currency' in Ghana."[158]
♠ Precious metals ♣ present ♥ Devisse (1988) commissioned an exact translation of Al Bakri's famous passages concerning gold which "brings a new solution to the to the interpretation of the pair tibr-dhahab." Ghali, the translator, found that tibr meant gold in rough state, compared to dhabab, which was gold in a worked state (refined gold). What do we make of Al Bakri's claim "the sovereign regulated the circulation of gold by keeping the nuggets, so that the metal did not depreciate through overabundance? ... The traditional distinction between nuggets and dust does not hold water. The real distinction is a different one: 'pure' gold, which by definition the ruler set aside for himself and which was intended for coinage, was dhabab." [159] However, gold not used for coinage: "no trace of a die or mint has been found south of the desert."[160]
♠ Foreign coins ♣ inferred present ♥ Cosmopolitan commerce centers: Timbuktu, Djenne, Biru, Soo, Ndob, Pekes and some others.[161] Currency "consisted of salt, cowries, or gold in either dust or pieces (of foreign or local mintage)."[162]
♠ Indigenous coins ♣ inferred present ♥ Gold not used for coinage: "no trace of a die or mint has been found south of the desert."[163] Currency "consisted of salt, cowries, or gold in either dust or pieces (of foreign or local mintage)."[164] According to Leo Africanus cowries used as currency for trading came from the Indian Ocean, via Persia.[165] According to al Bakri (11th century) 'The dinars they used were of pure gold and were called sola [bald] because they bore no imprints.' ... Thus these documents allow us to be sure of the use in Black Africa of imprinted gold coins, without, however, being able to know whether such imprints were effiges of local emperors or kings, or to know whether there was any generalized imperial currency minited apart from the mitkal standard."[166] According to al Bakri (11th century) square textile currency called chigguiya used at Silla.[167] Other currencies copper rings, and a cereal called dora.[168]
♠ Paper currency ♣ inferred absent ♥
♠ Debt and credit structures ♣ inferred present ♥ At this time, Africa was very advanced commercially. In the 10th century, Ibn Hauqal was very surprised to see a written acknowledgement of debt in the hands of a merchant from Aoudaghost, which is significative. "A cette époque, l'Afrique est en avance dans le domaine commercial. L'étonnement d'Ibn Hauqal au Xème siècle à la vue d'une reconnaissance de dette entre les mains d'un marchand d'Aoudaghost est à cet égard significatif." [169] Ibn Haukal (10th century) "attests to the use of acknowledgement of debts in writing".[170]
♠ Store of wealth ♣ inferred present ♥

Animals used in warfare[edit]

♠ Horses ♣ present ♥ "The earliest irrefutable evidence of horses in sub-Saharan Africa comes from the Arabic texts, beginning with the writings of Al-Muhallabi from about AD 985. By then, however, the horse was a highly valued prestige animal, and camels were the vehicle of trans-Saharan trade."[184] Soninke "acquired small horses brought from North Africa." The Soninke's possessed "superior iron weapons and horses" [185]
♠ Camels ♣ inferred present ♥ "Berber-speaking forebears of the present-day Tuareg are believed to have introduced camels to the Saharan trade routes, sometime between the second and fifth centuries AD... Camels extended both the volume and the radius of trade." "The Sanhaja people of the Western Sahara acquired large numbers of camels by the fourth and fifth centuries." [186]

♠ Shields ♣ present ♥ "Behind the king stand ten pages holding shields and swords decorated with gold..." [189]
♠ Helmets ♣ [absent; present] ♥ Knights: "The princes of Black Africa who could afford to outfitted themselves in complete or partial armor like that of the knights of the Western Middle Ages."[190] "coat of mail and iron breastplate, helmet, boots, javelin ... all of it."[191] However, due to climate complete knightly armour not as common as in Europe and in fact Songhai Askia Bano died of suffocation.[192]

Naval technology[edit]

♠ Small vessels (canoes, etc) ♣ present ♥ dugout canoes very old technology in West Africa - oldest found at Dufuna at least 6,400 ago.[208]

Fortifications[edit]

♠ Settlements in a defensive position ♣ present ♥ Built on a plain, the city was defended by a belt of hills that enabled it to control various points of access. This was the case of Niani, the ancient capital of Mali: located in a vast plain near the Sankarani, it was protected by a ring of hills leaving passageways between them. "Bâtie au milieu d'une plaine, la ville était défendue par une ceinture de collines qui permettaient un contrôle facile des voies d'accès. C'est le cas notamment de Niani, l'ancienne capitale du Mali: située au milieu d'une vaste plaine au bord du Sankarani, elle était protégée par un arc de cercle de collines laissant entre elles de larges passages." [210]
♠ Wooden palisades ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Earth ramparts ♣ [absent; present] ♥ Djenne had been "fortified by a system of ramparts, with a variable number of guarded gates. A fortified city was called a tata."[211]
♠ Ditch ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Moat ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Stone walls (non-mortared) ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Stone walls (mortared) ♣ inferred present ♥ According to Al Bakri, emperor of Ghana "lived in a stone castle, surrounded by a wall."[212] Idrisi, writing in 1150 CE, said it was a "fortified chateau, built in 1116, decorated with sculptures and paintings, and boasting glass windows."[213]

Other technologies[edit]

Military Organization[edit]

♠ Standing armies ♣ absent ♥ Only reference to professional and standing army in early West Africa is Askia Muhammed Toure (r.1493-1529 CE) of the Songhai Empire who "created a professional full-time army" [214] and "standing army"[215] before Askia Muhammad of Songhai Empire "Chiefs, kings and emperors of earlier times had relied on simply 'calling up' their subjects, their vassals, or their allies. ... But these were temporary armies. They were amateur armies. They served for a campaign or a war, and then everyone went home again until the next one."[216]

According to al-Bakri, the king of Ghana could "command the servces of many descent-lines. He could raise big armies."[217]

♠ Uniformity of equipment ♣ absent ♥ Only reference to professional and standing army in early West Africa is Askia Muhammed Toure (r.1493-1529 CE) of the Songhai Empire who "created a professional full-time army" [218] and "standing army"[219]

♠ Total army ♣ {[10,000-20,000]; [100,000-200,000]} ♥ "One chronicler reported that 'the king of Ghana, when he calls up his army, can put 200,000 men into the field, more than 40,000 of them archers.' How the men were assembled, fed and deployed is not revealed. The figures probably are a tenfold, or even a twentyfold exaggeration."[220]

According to al Bakri, 200,000 warriors, 40,000 of whom were archers.[221]

♠ Professional army ♣ ♥ '

"One chronicler reported that 'the king of Ghana, when he calls up his army, can put 200,000 men into the field, more than 40,000 of them archers.' How the men were assembled, fed and deployed is not revealed. The figures probably are a tenfold, or even a twentyfold exaggeration."[222]

According to al-Bakri, King Tunka Manin could place over '200,000 warriors in the field, more than 40,000 of them beig armed with bow and arrow."[223] However Davidson (1998) says "This was surely an exaggeration: the real strength of the Ghana armies, as we know from other North African sources, came from their power in iron-pointed spears."[224]

♠ Largest fielded army ♣ ♥ "One chronicler reported that 'the king of Ghana, when he calls up his army, can put 200,000 men into the field, more than 40,000 of them archers.' How the men were assembled, fed and deployed is not revealed. The figures probably are a tenfold, or even a twentyfold exaggeration."[225]

"les royaumes vassaux tels que Sosso, Diara et le Tékrour conservaient leur organisation initiale et se contentaient de verser un tribut annuel et d’apporter leur contribution sur le plan militaire en fournissant à l’empereur un contingent." the vassal kingdoms such as Sosso, Diara and Tekrour retained their initial organization and were happy to pay an annual tribute and to contribute militarily by providing the Emperor a quota.[226]

General Characteristics of Warfare[edit]

Types of Warfare[edit]

♠ Inter-polity wars ♣ present ♥ Almoravids, land of Barbara and Amima.

♠ Internal wars ♣ present ♥ En effet l’empire était miné de l’intérieur par des conflits de succession, les vassaux supportaient de plus en plus mal la domination du Ghana: ils aspiraient à prendre leur indépendance." In fact the empire was undermined from within by succession disputes, vassals endured more bad Ghana domination: they aspired to become independent. [227]

In fact the empire was undermined from within by succession disputes, vassals endured more bad Ghana domination: they aspired to become independent. [228]

♠ Wars of world religion against tribal religion ♣ inferred present ♥ Ghana "was not a Muslim state despite the presence of a small cadre of Muslism at the king's court as part of the administrative apparatus."[230]

♠ Wars on Urbanism-Literacy frontier ♣ suspected unknown ♥ land of Barbara and Amima? "The people of Ghana make raids on the land of Barbara and Amima and capture their people as they used to do when they were pagans. Amima are a tribe of Janawa who live on the coast of the Great Sea in the west... They follow the religion of the Majus. On account of their paganism no one enters their country and no merchandise is imported into it. ... The people of Ghana make raids on them every year. Sometimes they conquer them and sometimes they are conquered. These people have no iron and fight only with clubs of ebony. For this reason the people of Ghana overcome them, for they fight them with swords and spears."[231]

♠ Wars on steppe frontier ♣ present ♥ Berbers.

Ritual variables[edit]

A collective ritual is a ritual in which two or more people participate. With the official cult we refer to the set of collective ritual practices that are most closely associated with legitimation of the power structure (including elites, if any).

♠ RA ♣ Enrico Cioni ♥

♠ Expert ♣ ♥

Largest scale collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Hajj ♥ Either through violent imposition or peaceful persuasion, the rulers of Ghana converted to Islam following contact with the Almoravids of North Africa [233]. Though the sources do not provide a description of how Islamic ritual was performed specifically in the Ghana Empire, it is likely that it would have been similar to how the Almoravids performed it--that is, abiding strictly by the holy scriptures and the oldest traditions, avoiding synchretism with indigenous traditions, and following Sunni rather than Shi'ite ways [234]. The largest-scale Islamic ritual is the Hajj, and it is possible that West African Muslim merchants would have had the resources and international contacts necessary to perform the arduous pilgrimage through the desert, though there are no records of this. It appears that the fundamental way in which the principal rituals of the Hajj are performed today has not changed significantly since the time of Mohammed [235], so it may be reasonably inferred that the following description would apply to Almoravid times. "The Hajj begins when the pilgrims cross the consecrated threshold of the Grand Mosque, which surrounds the Ka'ba, separating the sacred from the profane. To come into the presence of the sanctuary, pilgrims must rid themselves of their ordinary clothes and don the sanctified garments--two seamless white pieces of cloth for men; any similar plain garment for women--that signify a state of purity (ihram). The men shave their heads and trim their beards and nails; the women clip a few locks of hair. "Once this sanctified state has been reached, the intention to perform the rites is voiced, and the pilgrim embarks on the tawaf: the seven circumambulations of the sanctuary, which still function as the principal ritual of the pilgrimage. [...] "Once the circumambulations are complete, the pilgrim moves on to a series of rituals that, according to tradition, were established by Muhammad in the last year of his life. These include running back and forth between the twin hills, Safah and Marwah, to commemorate Hagar's search for water; traveling to Mt. Arafat (the refuge of Adam and Eve after they were exiled from Eden, and the site of Muhammad's first sermon); the stoning of three pillars at Mina, which represent the Devil; and finally, the sacrifice of sheep, cows or lambs to mark the end of the pilgrimage (the meat is then distributed to the poor). When the rites are complete, the pilgrim removes the sanctified garments and reenters the world of the profane as a Hajji; the next time the garments are worn will be as a burial shroud" [236].
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ The main acts of the Hajj take place in the twelfth month of the Islamic calendar, although "a pilgrim can start going into consecration (ihram) for Hajj earlier, from the beginning of the tenth month (Shawwal)" [237].
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ once in a lifetime ♥ Every Muslim should undertake the Hajj pilgrimage at least once in their lifetime [238].
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ yearly ♥ The inhabitants of Mecca, Medina and other important waypoints along the pilgrimage may be considered as "passive spectators or witnesses", as the Seshat codebook puts it. They would witness the Hajj, or parts of it, between the tenth and the twelfth months of the Islamic calendar, every year [239].
♠ Duration ♣ 120 ♥ hours. Five days. The main acts of the Hajj take place in five days of the twelfth month of the Islamic calendar, that is, from the 8th to the 13th of the month of Dhu'l Hijja: however, "a pilgrim can start going into consecration (ihram) for Hajj earlier, from the beginning of the tenth month (Shawwal)" [240].
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Not specified by sources.
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ elites ♥ Religious specialists would have been Muslims, and the Caliphate's rulers were Muslims, which means that religious specialists came from the dominant religious group.
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ majority ♥ all Muslims [241]
♠ Inclusiveness (audience) ♣ other ♥ The inhabitants of Mecca, Medina and other important waypoints along the pilgrimage may be considered as "passive spectators or witnesses", as the Seshat codebook puts it. They would witness the Hajj, or parts of it, between the tenth and the twelfth months of the Islamic calendar, every year [242]. (KB: Copied evidence from audience frequency)

Most widespread collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ salat al jum a ♥ Salat al-jum'a. Either through violent imposition or peaceful persuasion, the rulers of Ghana converted to Islam following contact with the Almoravids of North Africa [275]. Though the sources do not provide a description of how Islamic ritual was performed specifically in the Ghana Empire, it is likely that it would have been similar to how the Almoravids performed it--that is, abiding strictly by the holy scriptures and the oldest traditions, avoiding synchretism with indigenous traditions, and following Sunni rather than Shi'ite ways [276]. The most widespread ritual, then, would have been salat. Salat is "the ritualized, obligatory prayer performed five times a day: sunrise, noon, afternoon, sunset, and evening. Salat, which means 'to bow, bend, or stretch,' is composed of a series of yogic movements that include standing, bowing, rising, sitting, turning east and west, and falling prostrate, all repeated in cycles, and accompanied by specific verses from the Quran. "As with all Muslim rituals, salat can begin only after the intention to pray is voiced, and only while the Muslim faces toward Mecca, the direction of prayer, or qiblah. Although salat can be performed individually as a means to purify and cleanse the soul, it is meant to be a communal act that binds the Ummah as a single body. For this reason, it is always preferable to perform salat in a place of assembly. Indeed, one particular salat--the noon prayer on Fridays (salat al-jum'a)--must be performed in the mosque, in the presence of the community. And while five daily prayers may seem like a heavy burden, the obligation is suspended for the sick, those who are traveling, or anyone else who is unable to perform them for any justifiable reason; if one wishes, missed salats can always be made up at a later time" [277]. Although salat can be performed individually as a means to purify and cleanse the soul, it is meant to be a communal act that binds the Ummah as a single body. For this reason, it is always preferable to perform salat in a place of assembly. Indeed, one particular salat--the noon prayer on Fridays (salat al-jum'a)--must be performed in the mosque, in the presence of the community. And while five daily prayers may seem like a heavy burden, the obligation is suspended for the sick, those who are traveling, or anyone else who is unable to perform them for any justifiable reason; if one wishes, missed salats can always be made up at a later time" [278].

Most euphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ Id al Fitr ♥ 'Id al-Fitr. Either through violent imposition or peaceful persuasion, the rulers of Ghana converted to Islam following contact with the Almoravids of North Africa [355]. Though the sources do not provide a description of how Islamic ritual was performed specifically in the Ghana Empire, it is likely that it would have been similar to how the Almoravids performed it--that is, abiding strictly by the holy scriptures and the oldest traditions, avoiding synchretism with indigenous traditions, and following Sunni rather than Shi'ite ways [356]. And because there are only two large festivals in the Islamic calendar, it makes sense to infer that one of these, the 'Id al-Fitr, was the most euphoric collective ritual of the official cult--although, as shall be seen from the following description, it was a euphoria tempered by the preceding fast. The 'Id al-Fitr is celebrated at the end of the month-long Islamic fast known as Ramadan. "As in the Friday afternoon congregational prayer of the weekly Day of Assembly, the Eid morning prayers at the mosque should be preceded by a bath and attended with clean (often new) clothes. After breakfast, men, women, and children gather at an outdoor prayer meeting, the musallah, in front of a white wall adorned with a niche, the mihraab pointing in the direction of Mecca (like the one in every mosque). Women are given the option of praying at home--as they mostly do the rest of the year. The prayers consist of two units (rakat) plus the six-to-sixteen additional takbir recitations of Allahu Akbar ('God is Most Great'). These set them apart from Friday prayers, as does the fact that they are followed instead of preceded by a two-part sermon (khutba). [...] This enthusiasm erupts spontaneously on the aptly named Eid-al-Fitr, the festival of the breaking of the month-long Ramadan fast. Still, by then, passions have been so well trained that few unbridled excesses are in evidence at the happy family celebrations of warm community that follow or at the fairground attractions that are set up in some places [...]. Indeed, restored social harmony is on display everywhere over up to three days, with people greeting and congratulating each other in the streets as well as on afternoon visits to relatives, friends, and sick people. Many gifts are offered, particularly to the children of less fortunate families. At home, the family celebrates around a noontime festive meal [...]. Dead relatives are also remembered as their graves are visited that day." [357] There is also a specific type of charity given on the occasion of 'Id al-Fitr, known as Zakat-ul-Fitr [358].

♠ Inclusiveness (audience) ♣ whole polity ♥ The 'Id al-Fitr is a public ritual [366].

Most dysphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ circumcision ♥ There are no records of the performance of dysphoric rituals in the Ghana Empire. However, circumcision is a widespread and apparently ancient rite of passage for boys throughout West Africa, and it is possible that it was already practiced in this polity. If so, it would probably have involved: an all-night dance preceding the ritual; the ritual surgery itself; and a more or less prolonged period of isolation for the boys, in which they are educated regarding their new status as adult men. There may also have been much singing in praise of their bravery. This is based on ethnographic observation of the Mande ethnic group [395].
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ yearly ♥ every few years. If similar to how the Mande practice this ritual [396].
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ once in a lifetime ♥ If similar to how the Mande practice this ritual [397].
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ yearly ♥ every few years. If similar to how the Mande practice this ritual [398].
♠ Duration ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Depends on how long it was deemed suitable for the boys to be kept in isolation after the surgery.
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ suspected unknown ♥ in absolute numbers. Not indicated by sources [399].
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ substantial minority ♥ Not indicated by sources, although among the Mande only members of the blacksmith caste may perform the ritual [400].
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ substantial minority ♥ teenage boys [401].
♠ Inclusiveness (audience) ♣ whole polity ♥ The dancing and surgery would have probably been public, as they are among the Mande [402].

♠ fear ♣ present ♥ Mande boys look forward to the event "with awe and fear" [405].
♠ humiliation ♣ absent ♥ Not recorded for this ritual among the Mande [406].
♠ disgust ♣ absent ♥ Not recorded for this ritual among the Mande [407].

♠ pain ♣ present ♥ [409]
♠ mutilation ♣ present ♥ The act of circumcision may be considered an act of mutilation [410].
♠ risk of death ♣ present ♥ "Heavy bleeding" is a great concern to smiths, as is "the likelihood of dangerous infection" among the Mande [411]. "Spiritual" dangers such as occult attacks on the boys (e.g. by a woman jealous of another woman's son), whose symptoms are "dizziness or light-headedness", and whose intention may be murder [412].

Limits on Power of the Chief Executive[edit]

Legal (formal) limits[edit]

♠ Executive power is separate or independent from military ♣ absent ♥ "In Mali and Songhai "the king appinted the generals was himself commander-in-chief of the army and personally directed military operations".[437] Askia Muhammad of Songahai Empire was in charge of a professional military: "Chiefs, kings and emperors of earlier times had relied on simply 'calling up' their subjects, their vassals, or their allies. ... But these were temporary armies. They were amateur armies. They served for a campaign or a war, and then everyone went home again until the next one."[438] "Ces trois grandes tribus guerrières - les fados ou classe de guerriers - formaient l'aristocratie au sein de laquelle étaient recrutés les hauts fonctonnaires et les grands dignitaires de la cour. Elles constituent la noblesse de race, car toutes sont issues de la grande famille des Wago dont le pays d'origine est Wagadou (il est à noter que la noblesse se dit wagheya)." These three great tribes of warriors - the fados, or warrior class- formed the aristocracy, from which state administrators and court dignitaries came from. They constitute the nobility through breeding, because all come from the Wago family, whose country of origin is Wagadou (it should be noted that the nobility is called wagheya).[439]
♠ Executive power is separate or independent from legislative ♣ inferred absent ♥ In reference to later Mali state a military aristocracy formed a council which advised the Emperor.[440] "Islam provided the kind of laws that were useful to bigger states and larger market-towns. Such laws were about the administration of justice, the inheritance of property, the improvement of market organization".[441] King of Ghana "held an audience for the people, to hear their grievances and remedy them".[442] It was not until Askia Mohammed of the Songhai Empire when Islamic law (of the cities) became dominant over traditional African law (of the countryside. Before Askia Mohammed the kings were lenient toward non-Islamic religions/laws.
♠ Executive power is separate or independent from judiciary ♣ [absent; present] ♥ King of Ghana "held an audience for the people, to hear their grievances and remedy them".[443] However, for later Mali Empire it seems that judicial power at the highest level may have been split. "Crimes of lese-majeste and high treason were under the jurisdiction of the king."[444] The cadi, a Muslim judge appointed by the king, "handled mainly common-law misdemeanors, disputes between citizens, or between citizens and foreigners."[445] Underscoring the independence of the judiciary, "A judge's house and a mosque were inviolable sanctuaries. The cadi could with impunity admonish the king; to the princes, he was the respected intercessor whose words were heeded."[446]

Legal (formal) freedoms[edit]

♠ Right to petition executive ♣ present ♥ According to al-Bakri the king of Ghana, under a pavilion, 'gives audience to his people, to listen to their complaints and to set them to rights',[447] "held an audience for the people, to hear their grievances and remedy them".[448]
♠ Critique of executive by government ♣ inferred present ♥ For later Mali Empire it seems that judicial power at the highest level may have been split. "Crimes of lese-majeste and high treason were under the jurisdiction of the king."[449] The cadi, a Muslim judge appointed by the king, "handled mainly common-law misdemeanors, disputes between citizens, or between citizens and foreigners."[450] Underscoring the independence of the judiciary, "A judge's house and a mosque were inviolable sanctuaries. The cadi could with impunity admonish the king; to the princes, he was the respected intercessor whose words were heeded."[451]
♠ Critique of executive by non-government ♣ inferred present ♥ Even by the 16th CE West African societies were not as stricly divided into classes compared to Europe and Asia. This is why vertical divisions (clans, descent-lines, communities) mattered more than horizontal divisions and "during the sixteenth century and onwards were much more democratic, much more respectful of the rights of individual persons, than were the governments of Europe."[452] "So long as they obeyed the laws of Ghana and paid their taxes, the traders from the north were sure of safety and hospitality."[453]

Customary (informal) checks on executive[edit]

♠ Informal critique of executive ♣ inferred present ♥ Even by the 16th CE West African societies were not as strictly divided into classes compared to Europe and Asia. This is why vertical divisions (clans, descent-lines, communities) mattered more than horizontal divisions and "during the sixteenth century and onwards were much more democratic, much more respectful of the rights of individual persons, than were the governments of Europe."[457] "There came a clash between Muslim law and traditional African law."[458] "So long as they obeyed the laws of Ghana and paid their taxes, the traders from the north were sure of safety and hospitality."[459]
♠ Informal sanction of executive ♣ inferred absent ♥
♠ Informal constraint on executive ♣ inferred present ♥ General reference to divisions in societies of West Africa "veritical divisions which separated all the people of one clan from the people of other clans, all the inhabitants of one village from their neighbours, all the descenants of one set of ancestors from the descendants of other ancestors. These vertical, or 'up-and-down', divisions were in some ways even more important to West African social development that the horizontal divisions ... and they were already very ancient by the sixteenth century."[460] "There came a clash between Muslim law and traditional African law."[461] Example of vertical divisions "entire groups were devoted to commerce", so according to the Tarikh el Fettach: 'know that the Uangaras and Malinkes share the same origin, but that Malinke is used to designate the warriors, whereas Uangara serves to indicate the merchants who carry on trading from one country to another.'[462] "In 990 the Berber center of the Lemtunas, Aoudaghast, was governed by a Black farba who levied taxes, tariff duties, etc., in the name of the Emperor on the goods and merchandise of the city's population, made up almost exclusively of Berbers and Arabs; these two groups moreover hated each other at the time."[463] "So long as they obeyed the laws of Ghana and paid their taxes, the traders from the north were sure of safety and hospitality."[464]
♠ Informal impeachment ♣ inferred absent ♥

Limits on executive power imposed by religious agents[edit]

♠ Critique of executive by religious agents ♣ present ♥ General reference to diviners and priests. [465] In Mali Empire there were diviners "whose job it was to predict the future."[466] The cadi was a Muslim judge appointed by the king who "handled mainly common-law misdemeanors, disputes between citizens, or between citizens and foreigners."[467] "A judge's house and a mosque were inviolable sanctuaries. The cadi could with impunity admonish the king; to the princes, he was the respected intercessor whose words were heeded."[468] Sovereign of Djenne was converted to Islam in the 12th century probably by ulema religious scholars.[469]
♠ Sanction of executive by religious agents ♣ inferred absent ♥
♠ Constraint on executive by religious agents ♣ present ♥ General reference to diviners and priests. [470] In Mali Empire there were diviners "whose job it was to predict the future."[471] Sometime later "There came a clash between Muslim law and traditional African law"[472] but this clash was always present.
♠ Constraint on executive by religious institutions ♣ present ♥ General reference to diviners and priests. [473] In Mali Empire there were diviners "whose job it was to predict the future."[474] Sometime later "There came a clash between Muslim law and traditional African law"[475] but this clash was always present.
♠ Impeachment by religious agents ♣ absent ♥
♠ Subordination of the chief executive to a religious agent ♣ absent ♥
♠ Motivation by belief in supernatural agent ♣ present ♥ "Like the Europeans of the Middle Ages (AD 600-1350), Africans lived in an 'age of faith'. They believed, in short, that political authority came not from men or women but from God and the spirits. Those who exercised power on earth could do so, in other words, only if they were accepted as speaking and acting with the good will of their departed ancestors, who, in turn, were their protectors and helpers in the world of the spirits. Rulers could only rule if they were spiritually appointed to do so; and their subjects obeyed them not simply from respect for the king's power and law, but also for reasons of religion."[476]

All others following the same religion as the Tunkara - i.e. the tradition - wore loincloth of cotton, silk, or brocade, according to their means."[477]

Central Bureaucracy[edit]

♠ State-level administrators ♣ inferred present ♥

The following Mali Empire was like ancient Ghana Empire but on a "greater scale"[478] but did not have a bureaucracy of the extent developed from Askia Mohammed of the Songhai Empire. Likewise the Ghana Empire would not have had a well-developed bureaucracy and what it did have in terms of official administration would have been more rudimentary compared to the Mali state. The Ghana Empire was a less structurally egalitarian society: Ibn Hawqal and Al-Bakri reported servants or slaves of the king would be sacrificed at royal funerals.[479] Islam, which prohibited human sacrifice, was not the religion of state rulers until 1077 CE.[480]

In the Mali state the head of government was called "Mansa"[481] and a military aristocracy formed a council which advised the Emperor.[482] In Ghana the king was known by the title of ghana[483] or by title of kaya maghan which meant 'lord of the gold'.[484] While I have found no specific reference to a governing institution such as an aristocratic council al-Bakri does mention the king's counsellors.

According to al-Bakri when the king of Ghana, under a pavilion, 'gives audience to his people, to listen to their complaints' the 'governor of the city is seated on the ground in front of the king, and all around him are his counsellors in the same position.'[485] When king of Ghana held audience with the people to hear "grievances and remedy them", "sat on the ground before the king and, all about, were the viziers, that is, his ministers, in the same position."[486]

"Ces trois grandes tribus guerrières - les fados ou classe de guerriers - formaient l'aristocratie au sein de laquelle étaient recrutés les hauts fonctonnaires et les grands dignitaires de la cour. Elles constituent la noblesse de race, car toutes sont issues de la grande famille des Wago dont le pays d'origine est Wagadou (il est à noter que la noblesse se dit wagheya)." These three great tribes of warriors - the fados, or warrior class- formed the aristocracy, from which state administrators and court dignitaries came from. They constitute the nobility through breeding, because all come from the Wago family, whose country of origin is Wagadou (it should be noted that the nobility is called wagheya).[487]

While there are no references for the possession of slave tribes by the kings of the ancient Ghana period, which may have been an invention from the following Mali Empire period, there is no reason to believe that in revenue raising terms the state was otherwise much different. In general terms, the West African states were ruled by a non-landowning nobility who lived on "the institution of a tax, first conceived of as a tithe, a ritual deduction on the wealth of all subjects".[488] The state gained revenue from taxation on merchants in gold coins or goods[489] and from customs duties on imports and exports, which had existed from the Ghanaian period.[490]

Was there any court bureaucracy before Songhai?: "It is difficult to realize that, for fifteen hundred years, the Tunkas, Mansas, and Askias simply issued verbal orders and got verbal accounts and reports in return. The customs activities of the border areas, based on what we have just said, would presuppose some kind of precise accounting, just as the payment of taxes and other fees would seem to imply the issuing of receipts, especially to the peripatetic merchants at the markets of Timbuktu, Djenne, etc."[491] Ibn Haukal (10th century) "attests to the use of acknowledgement of debts in writing".[492]

Written language was the culture of an urban élite, that did not absorb surrounding cultures and languages.[493] The development of the bureaucracy in the Western Sudan was linked to arabic writing and the acceptance of Islamic religion. The oldest example of writing in West Africa is from a 1100 CE tomb inscription at Gao.[494] "Islam provided the kind of laws that were useful to bigger states and larger market-towns. Such laws were about the administration of justice, the inheritance of property, the improvement of market organization".[495]

Court ceremony very similar throughout Black Africa: covering head with dust presented a sign of humility.[496] At audiences with the king in ancient Ghana people poured dust over their heads.[497] Only the king wore a headdress at royal audiences.[498] The chief's rank obligated him to speak softly so "the Mansa of Mali, like the Askia, and present-day marabouts, all had their heralds who audibly transmitted to the assembly the words of the chief."[499]

While the later Songhai bureaucracy had officials dressed with a "distinctive uniform and insignia"[500] according to Al Bakri, in Ghana "only the king and his sister's son, that is, the newphew who is heir apparent, were allowed to wear tailored clothes. All others following the same religion as the Tunkara - i.e. the tradition - wore loincloth of cotton, silk, or brocade, according to their means."[501]

Al-Bakri described what might be an incipient bureaucratic center: "The king has a palace and a number of domed dwellings all surrounded with an enclosure like a city wall. ... The king's interpreters, the official in charge of his treasury and the majority of his ministers are Muslims."[502] According to al-Bakri, the king of Ghana could "employ large numbers of messengers and other servants."[503] One of the king's counsellors may have been a finance minister responsible for revenue and taxes. There also may have been officials and translaters to coordinate directly with the foreign merchants whom the king of Ghana permitted residence in a special town.

"the advantages of the long-distance and trans-Saharan trade easily persuaded him to allow the Muslim visitors to build and inhabit a town of their own."[504] Indeed the capital of the Ghana Empire was likely near this town. "In the course of Ghana's long history, the king's capital was undoubtably moved from one place to another."[505] The last capital was Kumbi Saleh, "about 320 kilometres north of modern Bamako. Here too there was a town where the king of Ghana lived, and another town nearby where the Muslim traders had their houses and stables."[506]

The court of the king of Ghana must have had strong links with merchants who sold gold and other goods. Wagadu was a strong commercial state as they were at a north/south trading crossroads on the border of the Sahara. "Their businessmen could obtain gold and ivory from their southern neighbours, and sell these goods to trans-Saharan and North African business partners."[507] Once trade cities which were complex enough to be centers for craftsmen (leather, wood, ivory, metal) established "city governments were needed, as well as men trained to be put in charge of keeping accounts, of maintaining law and order, of looking after the safety of citizens. Then the rulers of these cities began to extend their power to ever wider regions of the neighbouring countryside."[508]

The following Mali Empire had a monopoly on gold production, especially lands of Wangara and Bambuk.[509] Likewise in ancient Ghana the king claimed all gold found in as his own and so monopolised production of gold.[510] More exactly, the king of Ghana "kept for himself only that share of the metal that was found formed into chunks" while "the king left to the people all the gold dust they could extract from the empire's mines."[511]

♠ Prestige of state-level bureaucracy ♣ lower ♥

Local-level officials (provincial, regional, civic administration)[edit]

♠ Number of regional administrative units ♣ ♥

♠ Local-level administrators ♣ inferred present ♥

General reference to West African states and empires: "Their rulers governed through a system of governors and sub-governors; and each of these had his own bureaucracy ... These office-holders or officials operated an early form of civil service. ... This started a long time ago, certainly in the days of Ancient Ghana."[534] "Each provincial governor in Black Africa was an image of the king, with his own small court."[535]

"In 990 the Berber center of the Lemtunas, Aoudaghast, was governed by a Black farba who levied taxes, tariff duties, etc., in the name of the Emperor on the goods and merchandise of the city's population, made up almost exclusively of Berbers and Arabs; these two groups moreover hated each other at the time."[536]

Davidson (1998) says, according to al-Bakri, "By 800, the king of Ghana was able make lesser kings or chiefs obey his laws and pay him taxes."[537] The larger state had provinces ruled by governors.[538]

"the advantages of the long-distance and trans-Saharan trade easily persuaded him to allow the Muslim visitors to build and inhabit a town of their own."[539] The trader's town, about 10km from the king's capital, was built mostly in stone rather than the local West African style of "hardened clay, thatch, and wooden beams".[540]

General reference for Ghana, Mali, Songhai: non-landowning nobility lived on "the institution of a tax, first conceived of as a tithe, a ritual deduction on the wealth of all subjects".[541] The king also raised revenue from a system of customs duties on imports and exports, which had existed from the Ghanaian period.[542] "The principal source of revenue for the sovereigns of Black Africa ... was gold extracted from mines."[543]

Legal System[edit]

♠ Multiple legal systems ♣ present ♥

"the advantages of the long-distance and trans-Saharan trade easily persuaded him to allow the Muslim visitors to build and inhabit a town of their own."[563] The trader's town, about 10km from the king's capital, was built mostly in stone rather than the local West African style of "hardened clay, thatch, and wooden beams".[564] "So long as they obeyed the laws of Ghana and paid their taxes, the traders from the north were sure of safety and hospitality."[565]

"Islam provided the kind of laws that were useful to bigger states and larger market-towns. Such laws were about the administration of justice, the inheritance of property, the improvement of market organization".[566]

"There came a clash between Muslim law and traditional African law."[567] anfara-kuma: "traditional judge in pre-Islamic times. It was a hereditary office always held by members of the Kuma clan. They were anfaras, or judges, whence the term anfara-kuma which came to mean the cadi in Songhai.[568]

♠ Courts are supported by the state ♣ ♥

Accessibility of the Legal System

Property Rights[edit]

♠ Private property (land) ♣ inferred present ♥ "The African king, however powerful, was easily persuaded that the soil did not belong to him".[612] "The sale of land, properly speaking, seems to have been unknown in traditional pre-colonial Africa. ... Instead of land having constituted a wealth beyond the reach of certain social categories, it was within everyone's grasp, with no need to forfeit one's freedom, like the serf bound to the soil, in order to make use of it, to 'possess' it. The slave had his own patch of ground; the stranger who just this morning came to the village would also get his. Expropriation of the sort seen in sixteenth-century Europe was unthinkable in the history of precolonnial Africa."[613] "Islam provided the kind of laws that were useful to bigger states and larger market-towns. Such laws were about the administration of justice, the inheritance of property, the improvement of market organization".[614]
